    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="edae28372b0f40c3aa70c44be693e67c">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '5f8e5b3c-7ebc-474b-b174-8aa9791bc0c8'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d12146811" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>As a sodium superionic conductor, Mn-rich phosphate of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Mn<sub>1.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> is considered as one of the promising cathodes for sodium-ion batteries owing to its good thermodynamic stability and high working voltage. However, Na<sub>3.4</sub>Mn<sub>1.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> is faced with low electronic conductivity, poor cycling stability and complex phase transition caused by multi-electron transfers, which limits its practical application. Herein, an anion-regulated strategy is proposed to optimize the Mn-rich Na<sub>3.4</sub>Mn<sub>1.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> phosphate cathode. After introducing F anions into the lattice, the rate performance is improved from 60.5 to 72.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 C. Ascribed to unique structure design, the reaction kinetics of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Mn<sub>1.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> are significantly improved, as demonstrated by cyclic voltammetry at varied scan rates and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique. The generated M-F bond inhibits Jahn–Teller effect with an improved cycle stability (85.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 5 C with 94.3% capacity retention). Interestingly, reaction mechanism of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Mn<sub>1.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> with the complex two-phase and solid solution reactions changes to the whole solid solution reaction after fluorine substitution, and leads to a smaller volume change of 5.41% during reaction processes, which is verified by in situ X-ray diffraction. This anion regulation strategy provides a new method for designing the high-performance phosphate cathode materials of sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202304046-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202304046-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Nowadays, electrochemical energy storage technology plays an important role under the background of sustainable development.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0001_R_d12146803e403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Small Methods Nat. Commun. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In electrochemical energy storage, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have aroused widespread concern in emerging markets and scientific research owing to abundant sodium resources and low price.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0002_R_d12146803e408" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Commun. Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, it faces slow Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics and large volume changes for host materials during Na<sup>+</sup> de/-intercalation process due to the large ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (102 pm).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0003_R_d12146803e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Eng. J. ACS Nano InfoMat">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is necessary to develop cathode materials with fast Na<sup>+</sup> transmission to pursue better electrochemical performance. Up to now, a variety of cathodes have been exploited such as layered oxide,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0004_R_d12146803e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Carbon Energy J. Am. Chem. Soc. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Prussian blue analogs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0005_R_d12146803e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and polyanionic compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0006_R_d12146803e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Electrochem. Energy Rev. Adv. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Considering that one main potential application area of SIBs is grid-scale energy storage, ultralong cycle life should be one most significant requirement. However, the Prussian blue analogs are confronted with poor thermal stability and toxicity of the CN group, and the oxides are subjected to unsatisfactory cycling performance owing to poor structural stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0007_R_d12146803e444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, it can be deduced that polyanionic compounds will be highly brilliant in the future for SIBs applications based on comprehensive electrochemical properties including high-rate capability, specific capacity and working voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0008_R_d12146803e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Sodium superionic conductor (NASICON) phosphate cathodes have a stable structure with the formula of A<sub>x</sub>M<sub>2</sub>(XO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (A is alkali metal cation, M is transition metal, X is P, etc.).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0009_R_d12146803e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Small">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The NASICON cathodes have attracted extensive attention to qualities of good structural stability, high safety, and small volume change during discharge/charging processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0010_R_d12146803e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Sci. J Mater Chem Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The strong induction effect of polyanion PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> can achieve higher working potential.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0011_R_d12146803e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Mater. Chem. A">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> More and more studies focus on Mn-based NASICON phosphate cathode because of the high voltage and low cost of Mn element.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0012_R_d12146803e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As for a hot cathode material, Na<sub>3.4</sub>Mn<sub>1.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> has considerable development potential in NASICON cathode materials based on the features of cheap and environmentally friendly for Mn and Ti elements.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0013_R_d12146803e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Eng. J.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>3.4</sub>Mn<sub>1.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> not only owns a high theoretical capacity with the multi-electron reaction but also possesses three high potential platforms of Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> (<span style="text-decoration:underline">≈</span>4.0&nbsp;V), Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>2+</sup> (<span style="text-decoration:underline">≈</span>3.6&nbsp;V), and Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> (≈2.1&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0014_R_d12146803e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, similar to other polyanionic materials, Na<sub>3.4</sub>Mn<sub>1.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> also has poor electronic conductivity owing to MnO<sub>6</sub>/TiO<sub>6</sub> octahedron separated by PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedron, which exhibits slow Na<sup>+</sup> dynamic characteristics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0015_R_d12146803e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nano Energy">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, it also faces Mn<sup>3+</sup> Jahn–Teller effect and the dissolution of manganese ions into electrolyte, which causes the deformation and collapse of crystal structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0016_R_d12146803e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Energy Lett.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, serious voltage hysteresis caused by Na<sup>+</sup> and Mn<sup>2+</sup> cations mixture is bad to form a stable lattice framework.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0017_R_d12146803e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Energy Lett.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, Na<sub>3.4</sub>Mn<sub>1.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> undergoes a complex phase transition process during multi-electron transfers process, which severely limits Na<sup>+</sup> migration kinetic and brings about huge bulk effects, severely impairing the rate performance and cycling stability of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Mn<sub>1.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. It is urgent to find a simple and efficient method to improve electrochemical performance for Na<sub>3.4</sub>Mn<sub>1.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> materials.</p>
         
         <p>Here, we designed a new strategy for Na<sub>3.4</sub>Mn<sub>1.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (abbreviated as M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>) material by fluorine substitution to prepare Na<sub>3.4-3x</sub>Mn<sub>1.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.8</sub>(PO<sub>4-x</sub>F<sub>x</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (abbreviated as M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>x</sub>, x = 0 and 0.02). As a result, M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> delivered 133.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, which is higher than 107.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>. In addition, it exhibited 91.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C after 1000 cycles with 94.3% capacity retention. The improvement of the parent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient after fluorine substitution has been confirmed by the galvanostatic intermittent titling technique (GITT) and cyclic voltage (CV) tests. This indicates that fluorine substitution effectively improved Na<sup>+</sup> migration kinetics in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>. In situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) showed that M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> material after fluorine substitution behaves as a single solid solution reaction, which is beneficial to improve sluggish kinetic caused by complex phase transformations. The CC//M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> full cell maintained 76.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> specific capacity after 1000 cycles at 5 C with 0.009 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> fade of per cycle, which implies its practical value in real life. Based on the above, it is confirmed that this strategy could impel the development of Mn-based phosphate cathode materials for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202304046-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202304046-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202304046-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202304046-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structure Characterization of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>x</sub> Materials</h3>
            
            <p>As proof of concept, Rietveld refinement analysis for XRD was performed to investigate crystal structure. The XRD Rietveld refinement of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>x</sub> (x = 0, 0.02) materials have been compared in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0001">1a,b</a>. M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>x</sub> materials have similar diffraction patterns, which can well conform to NASICON structure of NMTP with rhombohedral <i>R-3c</i> space groups in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0015_R_d12146803e743" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nano Energy">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The specific effects of fluorine substitution on lattice parameters and dimensions are studied through Rietveld refinement. The rhombohedral cell with <i>R-3c</i> space group is used as an optimization model. Due to the low <i>R<sub>wp</sub></i> value (11.389% for M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> and 15.139% for M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub>), it is considered to be effective and reliable. More detailed crystallographic data of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> and M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub>, such as lattice parameters, atomic coordinates, and occupation, are shown in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1–S3</a> (Supporting Information). Fluorine was successfully substituted into crystal structure of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> material and changed crystal cell parameters. The cell parameter <i>c</i> of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> is 21.6919 Å, which is obviously smaller than the 21.8182 Å of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>. Similarly, the <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> value (<i>a</i> = <i>b</i>) of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> (8.8489 Å) is slightly smaller than that of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> (8.8759 Å). The unit cell volumes of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> and M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> are 1470.99 and 1488.58 Å<sup>3</sup>, respectively, implying fluorine substitution induces shrinkage of lattice at <i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, and <i>c</i> directions. The reason for crystal structure shrinks is that F<sup>−</sup> has stronger electronegativity and smaller ion radius compared with O<sup>2−</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0018_R_d12146803e840" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Energy">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The crystal structure of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> and M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> are composed of PO/F<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra and Mn/TiO<sub>6</sub> octahedra and those connected with each other by corner-sharing O/F atom of PO/F<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0001">1c</a>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0019_R_d12146803e866" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 3D open framework with a large interstitial site is beneficial to the fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport. Due to the different oxygen environment around Na atoms, Na<sup>+</sup> haves two different sites in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>.&nbsp;The fixed Na(1) site has a sixfold coordination, stronger binding with oxygen, and generally negligible electrochemical activity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0017_R_d12146803e880" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Energy Lett.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While, the Na(2) site has an eightfold coordination and weak binding ability with oxygen atom, which can realize reversible Na<sup>+</sup> de-/intercalation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0020_R_d12146803e887" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After introduction of fluorine into the crystal lattice of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>, the occupation of Na sites was changed significantly. The occupancy of Na(2) site increased from 0.858 to 0.861, the occupancy of Na(1) site decreased from 0.830 to 0.760. As a result, the active Na<sup>+</sup> in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> is increased remarkably, predicting that the specific capacity will also be significantly improved. Due to strong electronegativity of fluorine, it can replace oxygen atom in phosphate, and also improve voltage platform of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0021_R_d12146803e910" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Storage Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202304046-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bb592825-5a1c-4583-90a9-37daf4f09724/adfm202304046-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/bb592825-5a1c-4583-90a9-37daf4f09724/adfm202304046-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/725507ef-684a-4e14-957e-1cd59899748b/adfm202304046-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bb592825-5a1c-4583-90a9-37daf4f09724/adfm202304046-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202304046-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202304046" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural characterization of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> and M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub>. XRD Rietveld refinement results of a) M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> and b) M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub>. c) Schematic illustration for crystal structure change from M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> to M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub>. d) TGA test of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub>. High-resolution XPS spectra and corresponding deconvolution results for the orbital peaks of e) Mn 2p and f) Ti 2p.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Thermos gravimetry (TG) curves of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0001">1d</a> to determine the carbon content. The TG curve of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> exhibits 1.27&nbsp;wt.% mass loss owing to water loss under 270 °C and 10.04% mass loss contributing to carbon combustion at 270–800 °C. The Fourier transform infrared spectrum (FTIR) was employed to detect chemical bonds for M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>x</sub> in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The asymmetric stretching vibration (<i>υ</i><sub>as</sub>) and bending vibration (<i>υ</i><sub>b</sub>) of PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedron can be observed at 1107, 1021, and 554 cm<sup>−1</sup>. Two characteristic peaks located at 620 and 579 cm<sup>−1</sup> correspond to the stretching vibration of MO<sub>6</sub> (M = Mn,Ti) octahedra. These results above were accordant to previous reports for Na<sub>3</sub>MnTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0022_R_d12146803e1023" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The XPS analysis was implemented to detect the surface elemental composition and valence states of two samples. In the high-resolution Mn 2p spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a</a>, Supporting Information), the sharp peaks centered at 653.9 and 642&nbsp;eV represent the electrons of Mn 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, which are characteristic of Mn<sup>2+</sup> species for M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> material. Two peaks at 465.6 and 459.9&nbsp;eV were assigned to the electrons in Ti 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and 2p<sub>3/2</sub> for Ti<sup>4+</sup> and another couple of peaks at 463.6 and 458.8&nbsp;eV were owing to the electrons for Ti<sup>3+</sup> 2P<sub>1/2</sub> and 2P<sub>3/2</sub> for Ti<sup>3+</sup> in Ti 2p spectrum. Therefore, Mn<sup>2+</sup>, Ti<sup>3+</sup>, and Ti<sup>4+</sup> were co-existent in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> material consistent with previous reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0023_R_d12146803e1069" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Mater. Today Energy.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The valence state of Mn and Ti in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> material is the same as that of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0001">1e,f</a>. As depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a</a> (Supporting Information), the existence of Na, Mn, Ti, P, O, F, and C elements in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> can be clearly observed. Besides, it is obvious that all orbital peaks moved to a higher energy binding in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> material, which may be owing to fluorine introduction. The high-resolution P 2p spectrum of two materials showed that P element has only one existence state existence of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4b</a>, Supporting Information). The sharp peaks located at 686.9&nbsp;eV assigned to the electrons in F 1s in the high-resolution F 1s spectrum, which can illustrate fluorine introduction in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> crystal structure (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4c</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202304046-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202304046-sec-0040-title">2.2 Morphology and Size of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>x</sub> Materials</h3>
            
            <p>The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) were used to investigate size and morphology. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> present an irregular shape with particle size nearly 1&nbsp;µm. The particle morphology of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> is composed with more smaller grains than M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>, which have a little reduction with the size of 400&nbsp;nm nearly in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0002">2a,b</a>. Surprisingly, not only does the particle size become smaller and uniform, but also fluorine substitution alleviates particle agglomeration to a certain extent. Such a small particles will be profit to electrochemical properties improvement. It can be clearly seen that M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> particle is coated with a carbon layer with 6&nbsp;nm. The distinct lattice fringe of 0.64&nbsp;nm is relevant to the (10-2) plane of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0002">2c</a>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0002">2d–k</a>, the mapping of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> indicates the presence of Na, Mn, Ti, P, O, F and C elements that distributed homogeneously in sample, which can be inferred successful fluorine substitution. The F element is also distributed uniformly in the M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> particles . The same result can confirm that all the above elements existed in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> by EDS in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202304046-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/06b65df8-bd44-4d79-9714-02adb443f866/adfm202304046-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/06b65df8-bd44-4d79-9714-02adb443f866/adfm202304046-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dc21acef-a22c-4c46-9d35-00e62c09cc56/adfm202304046-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/06b65df8-bd44-4d79-9714-02adb443f866/adfm202304046-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202304046-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202304046" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphology Characterization of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub>. a) SEM. b) TEM. c) HRTEM. d–k) SEM and element mapping of Na, Mn, Ti, P, O, F, and C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202304046-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202304046-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Characterizations of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>x</sub></h3>
            
            <p>To study of fluorine substitution, the electrochemical performances of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>x</sub> electrodes were evaluated half cells at potential range of 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) with the coin-type cell. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0003">3a</a>, there are three couples of well-defined redox peaks (A1/C1, A2/C2, and A3/C3 in the potential ranges of 4.3–3.7, 3.7–2.5, and 2.5–1.5&nbsp;V) in both CV patterns. These three potential areas from high to low were ascribed to Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup>, Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Ti<sup>3+</sup>/Ti<sup>4+</sup> redox reactions with three steps transition of Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion. Differently, M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> exhibits the higher potential than M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> for each corresponding redox peaks. It is exciting that, M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> owned the evidently stronger peaks with larger peak areas than M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>, implying that fastest Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics behaviors are significantly improved after fluorine substitution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0024_R_d12146803e1293" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Storage Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is also confirmed by galvanostatic charge and discharge (GCD) tests in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0003">3b</a>. The GCD curves of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> and M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> are compared in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0003">3b</a> at 0.1 C (1 C = 176&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>). The three potential platforms from high to low are corresponds to the three redox couples of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup>, Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Ti<sup>3+</sup>/Ti<sup>4+</sup>, which was consistent with CV results. The capacity of Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> potential platforms had slightly improved owing to inductive effect of fluorine substitution. This is mainly due to high electronegativity of fluoride element, which forms transition metal element (M) and fluorine element (M-F) bonding, thus improving the redox potential of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0025_R_d12146803e1344" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> possesses an improved potential platforms and a specific capacity with 131.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C. Besides, coulombic efficiency of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> is 96% higher than that of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> (95.1%).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202304046-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5506bdac-fcb3-401d-a460-260c9c926dc9/adfm202304046-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5506bdac-fcb3-401d-a460-260c9c926dc9/adfm202304046-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ae88f462-eca7-4293-ad32-605e2c9d473c/adfm202304046-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5506bdac-fcb3-401d-a460-260c9c926dc9/adfm202304046-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202304046-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202304046" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>x</sub> materials. a) CV curves at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>; b) GCD curves at 0.1 C. c) The values of discharge specific capacity values and capacity ratios of three voltage intervals calculated from discharge curves in (b). d) Rate capability from 0.1 to 20 C. The GCD curves of e) M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> and f) M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> under different rates. g) Long cycling performance at 5 C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The whole potential range are divided into three parts of 4.3–3.7, 3.7–2.5, and 2.5–1.5&nbsp;V, which represented the different process of three steps transition separately. The discharge specific capacity and capacity ratios of three voltage intervals calculated from the discharge curves are showed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0003">3c</a>. Fluorine substitution is beneficial to capacity contribution and proportion of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> and Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> redox reaction in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> material. Interestingly, the capacity contribution of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> for total capacity improved from 18.9% to 22.4% by contrast of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> (19.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> (29.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). The boost of potential platforms and specific capacity giving rise to Mn element can achieve higher energy for practical application in full cell. For Ti<sup>3+</sup>/Ti<sup>4+</sup> redox reaction, fluoride substitution is beneficial to specific capacity of Ti element. To deeply analyze and estimate the electrochemical properties of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>x</sub> electrodes, the rate and cycling performance were intensively introduced in SIBs. It can be deduced that fluoride substitution of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>x</sub> can improve rate performance in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0003">3d</a>. With the current density increasing to 20 C, the specific capacity of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> cathode decreased seriously to 60.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0003">3e</a>. By contrary, M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> shows better rate performance by fluoride substitution, which delivered 133.2, 118.1, 111.9, 105.3, 100.4, 93.9, 85.7, 80.6, and 72.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, 15, and 20 C in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0003">3f</a>, respectively. M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> material not only maintained high specific capacity values but also exhibited better voltage platform retention than M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>, especially at high rates beyond 5 C. Fluorine substitution is conducive to rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transmission in materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0024_R_d12146803e1501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Storage Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> exhibits smaller polarization than M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> when the rate reaches 1 C, especially for the potential platforms nearly at ≈3.6 and 4.0&nbsp;V. This indicates that fluorine substitution is beneficial for reducing the polarization of reaction process and achieving faster ion transport. As the rate increases, M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> and M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> had an obvious inclined platform in 2.2–2.5&nbsp;V, which is attributed to voltage hysteresis caused by Na<sup>+</sup>/Mn<sup>2+</sup> mixture.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0017_R_d12146803e1532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Energy Lett.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cycling performance of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>x</sub> electrodes and corresponding GCD curves are compared in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information). M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> showed 91.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with 93.3% capacity retention at 2 C after 500 cycles, which is higher than 84.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>. Even at high rate of 5 C, M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> maintained 85.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> The GCD curve also indicates that fluorine substitution increases the capacity of high voltage platforms and reduces their rapid attenuation in high voltage platforms, indicating that fluorine substitution to some extent alleviates the Mn<sup>3+</sup> Jahn–Teller effect in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information), after 1000 cycles with 94.3% capacity retention, better than M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0003">3g</a>. This demonstrated that fluoride substitution can effectively reduce capacity attenuation and ameliorate kinetics of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-based cathode materials. The M-F bond produced alleviated Jahn–Teller effect of Mn<sup>3+</sup> , thus improving cyclic stability of original material.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202304046-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202304046-sec-0060-title">2.4 The Kinetics Behavior of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>x</sub> Electrodes</h3>
            
            <p>Furthermore, CV at various scanning rates and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was implemented to have an insight into kinetics behavior by fluoride substitution in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>x</sub> materials. The peak current evolution of CV curves is relevant to dynamic properties of Na<sup>+</sup> de/-insertion. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0004">4a,b</a> shows CV curves of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> and M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> electrodes under various scanning rates with 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V. For all samples, the increase of current value (<i>i<sub>p</sub></i>) of redox peaks can be clearly observed caused by scanning rate increase. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information), the linear relationship between <i>i<sub>p</sub></i> and <i>ν<sup>1/2</sup></i> fit linearly well, illustrating the diffusion-controlled properties of Na<sup>+</sup> de/-intercalation reaction in all materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0026_R_d12146803e1652" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Sci. Bull. Adv. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> material shows larger slope values than M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> by the linear relationship between <i>i<sub>p</sub></i> and <i>ν<sup>1/2</sup></i>, implied M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> has a fast dynamic behavior. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0004">4c</a> shows the comparison of <i>D<sub>app,Na</sub></i> values of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>x</sub> were calculated from the CV tests at various scan rates and its specific value is shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information)<sub>.</sub> Overall, the two redox processes Ti<sup>3+</sup>/Ti<sup>4+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> have fast reaction kinetics. Analyzing the date, it can be clearly found that fluoride substitution can improve apparent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient, illustrating fluorine substitution can effectively improve reaction dynamics and realize fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport. However, the kinetic improvement of fluorine substitution for different redox processes is different. The A1 and C1 peaks belong to Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> oxidation and reduction process were improved 1.08 ×&nbsp;10<sup>−10</sup> and 1.68 ×&nbsp;10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, which is the most improved in all peaks. Fluorine substitution is conducive to realizing rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transmission in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub>, thus improving rate performance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202304046-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0f4b5927-b09a-4c57-9d0b-61cb8a86cbae/adfm202304046-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0f4b5927-b09a-4c57-9d0b-61cb8a86cbae/adfm202304046-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ce18891f-ae0a-46d8-a521-c44739dc82f3/adfm202304046-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0f4b5927-b09a-4c57-9d0b-61cb8a86cbae/adfm202304046-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202304046-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202304046" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Studies of kinetics properties of Na<sup>+</sup> transportation in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>x</sub>. CV curves at various scan rates from 0.1 to 2.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> of a) M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> and b) M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub>. c) The comparison of <i>D<sub>app,Na</sub></i> values calculated from the CV tests at various scan rates. The GITT test results of d) M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> and e) M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> electrodes showing the variations of calculated <i>D<sub>app,Na</sub></i> in the charge and discharge process.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics properties of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> made further efforts to verify and estimate through GITT tests. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0004">4d,e</a> shows the variation of <i>D<sub>app,Na</sub></i> values along with GCD curves of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> and M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub>. The lower <i>D<sub>app,Na</sub></i> correspond to the potential plateau is due to the strong interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and host structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0027_R_d12146803e1832" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Small">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It shows that the kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> de/-insertion process in plateau area is slow, which is rate determining step of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. At the end of charge and discharge process, lower <i>D<sub>app,Na</sub></i> values mean the end of phase transition. It can be seen that <i>D<sub>app,Na</sub></i> values of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> are in the range of 10<sup>−9</sup> to 10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, higher than M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> with 10<sup>−9</sup> to 10<sup>−13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>. M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> shows more stable and higher D<sub>app,Na</sub> values in the platform area, especially for Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox reaction process nearly 4.0&nbsp;V. The average <i>D<sub>app,Na</sub></i> value in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> is significantly higher than that in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>, indicating that fluoride substitution can promote Na<sup>+</sup> dynamic characteristics in charging and discharging process. According to CV and GITT test results, it is found that M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> showed an excellent apparent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient. The improvement of kinetics properties of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> material after fluoride substitution further explains the reason for the improvement of rate performance of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> based materials.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202304046-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202304046-sec-0070-title">2.5 The Structural Change of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>x</sub> Electrodes</h3>
            
            <p>In situ XRD was carried out to explore Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> by fluorine substitution. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0005">5a,b</a> shows in situ XRD patterns of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> and M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> cathodes during first dis/-charging process in the voltage range of 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V. The (103), (204), and (116) peaks of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> shifted to high angle direction during the charging process, indicating that Na<sup>+</sup> is removed from crystal structure and made its shrink. The discharge process is opposite direction for its peaks moving, indicating that Na<sup>+</sup> is inserted into crystal structure and made crystal expand. These peaks are continuous changes during dis/-charging process, corresponding solid solution reaction mechanism. The same phenomenon occurred in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub>, indicating that it also had a solid solution reaction mechanism. Surprisingly, the (211) and (300) peaks in two samples showed great changes. For M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>, its (211) peak gradually disappears during charging process and gradually appeared during discharge process. The (300) peak will gradually split into two peaks and then restored to original state during dis/-charging process. The change of (211) and (300) peaks shows that M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> had a two-phase reaction. However, the (300) peak of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> after fluorine substitution does not split, only showed peak shift, corresponding to solid solution reaction. Based on above analyses, it is that Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> includes two-phase reaction and solid solution reaction, while M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> only had a single reaction (solid solution reaction). For M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>, the complex phase transition caused by multi-electron transfer process delayed Na<sup>+</sup> migration kinetics, thus showing poor rate performance. However, the fluorine substitution strategy makes M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> show a single solid solution reaction mechanism reducing the complex phase transformation process, thus improving the Na<sup>+</sup> migration kinetic , which corresponds to the kinetics and rate test results. In order to better explain crystal structure change in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> based materials, we calculated the lattice parameters and cell volume of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> and M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> materials as demonstrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0005">5c,d</a>. The lattice parameter a in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> and M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> gradually decreased and then increased in charging and discharging process, indicating that crystal structure first shrank and then expanded along a-axis direction. The lattice parameter <i>c</i>-axis of two materials shows an overall trend of increasing during the charging process, while it shows a trend of decreasing during the discharge process. As for cell volume, Na<sup>+</sup> is removed from the material during the charging process, resulting in crystal volume reduction of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> and M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub>, while the discharge process is opposite. 5.41% volume changes in M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> is smaller than 5.56% in original material in the charge process. Therefore, the complex phase transformation causes M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> cathode to undergo greater structural changes, which may not be conducive to the realization of long-term stability. In contrast, the structural change of fluorine substituted M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> is relatively small, indicating the fluorine substitution strategy can enhance the structural stability of material, thus helping to achieve better cycle performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0018_R_d12146803e2078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Energy">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The function of fluorine substitution is clearly shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0005">5e</a>. The fluorine substitution strategy reduces the complex phase transitions during the charging and discharging process of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>, alleviates the drastic changes in the crystal structure of material, and achieves better cycle performance. CV and GITT&nbsp;tests also prove that fluorine substitution improves the kinetics of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>, enabling rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transport under high-rate conditions, thereby reflecting the improvement of rate performance. The improvement of kinetics and the simplification of phase transformation process have realized the optimization of material properties as a whole.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202304046-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ad400520-da94-4c8f-9432-fcab3452dfff/adfm202304046-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ad400520-da94-4c8f-9432-fcab3452dfff/adfm202304046-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/21198bc5-ccfb-4f39-b7e6-30f53e310564/adfm202304046-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ad400520-da94-4c8f-9432-fcab3452dfff/adfm202304046-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202304046-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202304046" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In-situ XRD patterns of a) M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> and b) M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> cathodes during first charging/discharging process in the voltage range of 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V. Changes of lattice parameters of c) M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> and d) M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> under different charging and discharging conditions. e) Schematic illustration of the effect of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics and structural change by fluorine substitution.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202304046-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202304046-sec-0080-title">2.6 Full Cell Application</h3>
            
            <p>For its excellent electrochemical performance in half SIBs, we fabricated full cell coupled with M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> cathode and carbon cloth (CC) anode to measure its electrochemical performance and estimate practical application. Two visible peaks can be clearly obvious at 20° and 43° in CC anode, which were correspond to (002) and (100) planes of hard carbon in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a</a> (Supporting Information). It has a typical amorphous carbon material and is woven from interwoven carbon microtubules (about 10–15&nbsp;µm in diameter) with a textile network structure in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b and S13</a> (Supporting Information). CC anodes have 247.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 25&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information), which are suitable for matching cathodes for full cell. The first five CV curves of full cell has a good reproducibility in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0006">6a</a>, which implied a highly reversible electrochemical reaction and verified by GCD curves in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0006">6b</a>. The charge/discharge capacities of full cell are 111/121.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with high initial coulombic efficiency of 109.3% at 0.1 C. The discharge specifical capacity and the coulombic efficiency of the CC//M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> SIFCs in the full-cell test were lower than those in the half-cell test. This was attributed primarily to the activation of full cell in the first cycle.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202304046-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202304046-bib-0028_R_d12146803e2187" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the fifth cycle, it also exhibits 117.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> specific capacity (based on cathode material mass calculation). As displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0006">6c,d</a>, the rate performance of CC//M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> full cell also delivers 88.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> specific capacity at 10 C. The coulombic efficiency is maintained nearly at 100% under different rate. When returned to 0.1 C, the specific capacity of full cell can recover 111.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, amount to 91.7% of initial discharge capacity (121.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). Furthermore, its initial discharge specific capacity can reach 104 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 C and still maintain at 99.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> with 95.9% capacity retention after 200 cycles in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a</a> (Supporting Information). Even at 5 C, it also delivered 76.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles with 0.009 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> fade of per cycle in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0006">6e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16b</a> (Supporting Information). More importantly, full cell can successfully light up 26 red Light Emitting Diode (LED) bulbs consisted of lightning pattern in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202304046-fig-0006">6e</a>. The excellent electrochemical performance of CC//M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> full cell proves its application prospect in electrochemical energy storage.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202304046-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b9da8621-e14f-4e65-ae4e-982e3c276c13/adfm202304046-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b9da8621-e14f-4e65-ae4e-982e3c276c13/adfm202304046-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bdd15214-e1e8-4109-be9e-a8594b718fe2/adfm202304046-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b9da8621-e14f-4e65-ae4e-982e3c276c13/adfm202304046-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202304046-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202304046" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The electrochemical performance of CC//M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> full cell. a) CV curves of initial five cycles at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) GCD curves at 0.1 C. c) Rate performance from 0.1 C to 10 C and d) corresponding GCD curves. e) The cycle performance at 5 C (inset: practical demonstrations of coin-type).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202304046-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202304046-sec-0090-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we adopted anion-regulated strategy to optimize Mn-rich M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> phosphate cathode by fluorine substitution, and further achieved electrochemical performance improvement. The XRD Rietveld refinement and mapping tests confirmed that fluoride successfully entered into the lattice of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub> material. The changes of electrochemical properties and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics after fluoride substitution were discussed in depth. M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> electrode delivers the high discharge specific capacity of 133.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, which improves 25.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> compared with capacity of M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>. It also exhibits 72.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at high-rate of 20 C, which is exceed to 12.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> compared with M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>. M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> electrode achieve 93.3% capacity retention after 500 cycles at 2 C. Fluorine substitution enhances the acceleration of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics by CV and GITT tests explanation. M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> shows a single solid solution reaction mechanism, which is beneficial to achieve fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport. Besides, CC//M<sub>1.2</sub>T<sub>0.8</sub>-F<sub>0.02</sub> full cell displays outstanding rate capability (121.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C and 88.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 C) and long cycle life (89.5% capacity retention after 1000 cycles at 5 C), which indicates better prospect for actual application. This strategy provides a novel method in anion substitution chemistry to design high-performance NASICON cathode materials in SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
